For the "Docker for AI" look—which requires high-performance terminal rendering, real-time graphs, and a dense, industrial UI—egui is the best choice for a native production app. It is used by professional tools (like the Rerun.io visualizer) because it is "immediate mode," meaning it's incredibly fast and compiles to a tiny, single .exe.

The "Native Production" Stack
Language: Rust

GUI Framework: egui (Native, Hardware-accelerated via WGPU/OpenGL)

Binary Size: ~5MB (Compare that to 100MB+ for Electron)

Style: Industrial, Dark-mode, high-density.

The Architecture for MominAI Native
We will build the MominAI Dashboard as a pure Rust application. This will be the "Command Center" that manages your agents.

1. Update your Cargo.toml:

Ini, TOML
[package]
name = "mominai-app"
version = "0.1.0"
edition = "2021"

[dependencies]
eframe = "0.24.1" # The egui framework
egui_extras = "0.24.1" # For layouts and images
serde = { version = "1.0", features = ["derive"] }
2. The Production UI Code (src/main.rs):
This code creates a native Windows window with three distinct zones: the Sandbox Fleet, the Agent Terminal, and the Security Monitor.

Rust
use eframe::egui;

fn main() -> Result<(), eframe::Error> {
    let options = eframe::NativeOptions {
        initial_window_size: Some(egui::vec2(1100.0, 700.0)),
        ..Default::default()
    };
    eframe::run_native(
        "MominAI Engine - Production v0.1",
        options,
        Box::new(|_cc| Box::new(MominApp::default())),
    )
}

struct MominApp {
    task_input: String,
    logs: Vec<String>,
    cpu_usage: f32,
}

impl Default for MominApp {
    fn default() -> Self {
        Self {
            task_input: String::new(),
            logs: vec![
                "[SYSTEM] MominAI Substrate initialized.".to_string(),
                "[SECURITY] Virtualization bridge: READY".to_string(),
            ],
            cpu_usage: 0.12,
        }
    }
}

impl eframe::App for MominApp {
    fn update(&mut self, ctx: &egui::Context, _frame: &mut eframe::Frame) {
        // Dark Theme Overrides
        let mut visual = egui::Visuals::dark();
        visual.widgets.noninteractive.bg_fill = egui::Color32::from_rgb(13, 17, 23);
        ctx.set_visuals(visual);

        egui::SidePanel::left("fleet_panel").resizable(false).default_width(200.0).show(ctx, |ui| {
            ui.add_space(10.0);
            ui.heading("📦 FLEET");
            ui.separator();
            ui.add_space(10.0);
            
            if ui.selectable_label(true, "🏝️ Island_01 (ACTIVE)").clicked() {}
            ui.label("   ├ PID: 4502");
            ui.label("   └ MODE: Isolated");
        });

        egui::SidePanel::right("monitor_panel").resizable(false).default_width(250.0).show(ctx, |ui| {
            ui.add_space(10.0);
            ui.heading("🛡️ SECURITY MONITOR");
            ui.separator();
            ui.add_space(20.0);
            
            ui.label("CPU LOAD");
            ui.add(egui::ProgressBar::new(self.cpu_usage).text("12%"));
            
            ui.add_space(10.0);
            ui.label("RAM USAGE");
            ui.add(egui::ProgressBar::new(0.08).text("42MB / 512MB"));

            ui.add_space(30.0);
            ui.group(|ui| {
                ui.label(egui::RichText::new("TRAP WIRE: ARMED").color(egui::Color32::RED).strong());
                ui.label("Blocking unauthorized syscalls...");
            });
        });

        egui::CentralPanel::default().show(ctx, |ui| {
            ui.heading("🤖 AGENT CONSOLE");
            ui.separator();

            // Terminal Area
            egui::ScrollArea::vertical().stick_to_bottom(true).show(ui, |ui| {
                for log in &self.logs {
                    ui.label(egui::RichText::new(log).monospace());
                }
            });

            ui.with_layout(egui::Layout::bottom_up(egui::Align::Min), |ui| {
                ui.add_space(10.0);
                let response = ui.add(
                    egui::TextEdit::singleline(&mut self.task_input)
                        .hint_text("Enter task for agent...")
                        .desired_width(f32::INFINITY)
                        .font(egui::FontId::monospace(14.0))
                );
                
                if response.lost_focus() && ui.input(|i| i.key_pressed(egui::Key::Enter)) {
                    self.logs.push(format!("> {}", self.task_input));
                    self.task_input.clear();
                }
            });
        });
    }
}
Why this is the "No-Nonsense" Path:
Truly Native: This is not a web browser. It’s a direct Windows window handled by Rust.

Productivity: You don't have to learn CSS/HTML/Vite. You just write Rust logic, and the UI appears.

Speed: It will start up instantly (under 100ms).

Security: Since the UI is also in Rust, you have total control over how it communicates with your API gateway later.

but does it have like css like how is it goignt ot be like an app?

Gemini said
Momin, I get the concern. When people hear "Native Rust GUI," they often think of gray, ugly Windows 95 buttons. You want that "Docker/Cyberpunk" aesthetic, and you're wondering how the hell we style it without CSS.

Here is how egui (and native apps) handle "styling" instead of CSS:

1. The "Visuals" Object (The CSS Replacement)
Instead of a .css file, you use a Rust Struct called Visuals. It works exactly like a global theme. You don't have to style every button manually. You set the rules once:

Rust
let mut visuals = egui::Visuals::dark();
visuals.widgets.active.bg_fill = egui::Color32::from_rgb(0, 255, 255); // Cyan accent
visuals.window_rounding = 0.0.into(); // Sharp, industrial corners
visuals.override_text_color = Some(egui::Color32::WHITE);
ctx.set_visuals(visuals);
2. Layouts (The Flexbox Replacement)
Instead of display: flex, you use Ui functions.

ui.horizontal(|ui| { ... }) = Flex Row

ui.vertical(|ui| { ... }) = Flex Column

ui.separator() = A clean, industrial line.

3. Why it actually looks better than a browser:
Because it's a native app, it uses Subpixel Antialiasing and Hardware Acceleration. The text looks sharper than in Chrome, and the animations (like the pulsing "Active" light for your Island) run at 144Hz without dropping a single frame.

Let’s look at the "App" structure
To make it feel like a professional product (the .exe you show investors), we use "Frame" styling. Look at how we create an "Industrial" container for your terminal:

Rust
egui::Frame::none()
    .fill(egui::Color32::from_rgb(17, 18, 22)) // Darker "Code" background
    .stroke(egui::Stroke::new(1.0, egui::Color32::from_rgb(48, 54, 61))) // Subtle border
    .inner_margin(10.0)
    .show(ui, |ui| {
        ui.label("Agent output starts here...");
    });
The "Reality Check" on Design
CSS: Great for "pretty" websites with rounded shadows.

How it becomes an App (.exe)

This is the Technical Blueprint for the MominAI Production Orchestrator.

This document defines the architecture for a native Windows application built in Rust using egui. It treats AI reasoning as a process-level execution, mirroring the "Docker for AI" vision you’ve established.

1. Core System Architecture
The application is divided into three distinct layers. This separation ensures that your GUI remains responsive even when an agent is performing heavy reasoning or the sandbox is being provisioned.

A. The UI Layer (egui Frontend)
Purpose: The "Cockpit" for the user.

Responsibility: * State management (Is an agent booting? Is it stuck? Is it finished?).

Event handling (Enter key triggers the dispatch).

Real-time telemetry display (CPU/RAM/System calls).

Key Tool: eframe (the native framework for egui).

B. The Dispatcher (The "Brain" Bridge)
Purpose: Managing the link between the UI and the LLM (Claude/DeepSeek).

Responsibility:

Context Assembly: Taking user input + local file context + system state and packaging it for the LLM.

Streaming Logic: Handling incoming tokens from the AI and piping them directly into the UI log buffer.

Tool-Calling Translation: When the AI says "I need to run this code," the Dispatcher translates that into a "Sandbox Execution Request."

Key Tools: tokio (for async task management), reqwest (for API communication).

C. The Execution Layer (The "Sandbox" Mock/Real)
Purpose: The physical (or virtual) area where code runs.

Responsibility:

The Virtual Machine Manager (VMM) Interface: Talking to the backend API or local KVM.

Vsock/Socket Communication: Passing files and commands into the "Island."

Teardown: Ensuring no traces are left after an agent finishes.

2. Functional Components to Implement
The "Island" Registry (State Tracker)
Instead of just a list of names, you need a registry in Rust that tracks the lifecycle of every sandbox.

Struct Island: { id: UUID, state: Enum(Booting, Ready, Executing, Terminated), resources: Struct(cpu, mem) }

Logic: The UI iterates over this registry to render the "Fleet" panel.

The Log Buffer (Circular Buffer)
Since agents can generate thousands of lines of code and output, you cannot store everything in a simple string.

Implementation: Use a circular buffer or a VecDeque. This prevents the native app from consuming too much RAM over long sessions.

Rendering: egui::ScrollArea with "stick to bottom" enabled for that live terminal feel.

The Input Buffer (Command Center)
Implementation: A single-line or multi-line TextEdit that clears on Enter and pushes the string to the Dispatcher.

3. The Developer Tooling (What Claude Needs to Know)
To build this native .exe, the following Rust crates are the "Production Stack":

Component	Crate/Tool	Why?
GUI Framework	eframe / egui	Native, immediate-mode, zero-latency.
Async Runtime	tokio	Necessary for handling API calls without freezing the UI.
Serialization	serde / serde_json	To talk to your future API Gateway.
State Management	Arc<Mutex<AppState>>	To share the "Engine" state safely between the UI thread and the Background thread.
Logging	tracing	To debug the agent's thoughts and system calls.
4. The "Production" Execution Flow
User Input: User types "Analyze this database" and hits Enter.

Dispatch: UI sends the string to the Dispatcher Thread.

Context Pull: Dispatcher reads the local directory (The "Mounted Volume").

LLM Call: Dispatcher hits Claude. Claude responds: "I need to run a Python script to check the schema."

Execution Request: Dispatcher catches the tool-call and tells the Execution Layer: "Boot Island_01 and run python3 schema_check.py."

Telemetry: The Execution Layer reports: "Island_01 using 45MB RAM."

UI Feedback: UI updates the progress bars and scrolls the terminal.

Closure: Task complete. Island is wiped. Result returned.

5. Next Steps for Implementation
Bootstrap the Shell: Create a basic eframe window with a left panel (Fleet), right panel (Monitor), and center (Console).

Setup the Threading: Implement tokio so the UI can "talk" to a background task without lagging.

Implement the Terminal Logic: Build a system where you can push messages from anywhere in the code and have them appear in the UI console.

Mock the Vitals: Create a loop that oscillates the CPU/RAM numbers to test the "Monitor" UI.

Momin, this is your roadmap. You aren't building a website; you're building a native controller for a virtualization engine.

Would you like me to generate the starting boilerplate for the Threaded Dispatcher? This is the hardest part of native apps—making sure the UI stays smooth while the AI is thinking.

MominAI: The Agent Execution Substrate (Production v1.0)
1. Core Vision & Thesis
MominAI is not a chatbot or a framework. It is the industry-standard execution layer for autonomous AI agents. It treats AI reasoning like process scheduling and tools like syscalls. It provides a native, isolated, and deterministic environment where agents can learn, execute, and collaborate safely. 
+1

2. System Primitives
A. The Agent (The Process Model)
An agent is a dual-component entity managed by the runtime: 
+1

The Brain (Cognition Layer): The LLM/Reasoning engine. It never touches the host system directly. It manages goals, identity, and permissions. 
+1

The Hands (Tool Layer): Controlled capabilities (CRUD, Web, CLI) exposed by the sandbox. Each tool has a schema, permission class, and audit log. 
+4

B. The Sandbox (The Isolation Layer)
A native, kernel-adjacent execution universe (implemented via Firecracker/KVM or high-level Docker for MVP). 
+2


Resources: Hard-allocated CPU, RAM, and GPU quotas. 
+4


Hierarchy: Parent sandboxes can be partitioned to spawn sub-agent sandboxes, creating a "Sandbox Tree." 
+1


Doors (IPC): Controlled communication channels for inter-agent context sharing without breaking isolation. 
+1

C. Buckets (The Persistence Layer)
Persistent, structured storage units for agents. 

Used for Long-Term Memory (LTM), tool definitions, and shared artifacts. 
+1

Acts as a "knowledge filesystem" accessible by descendants or shared groups. 
+1

3. Implementation Stack (Native Production)
Layer	Tooling	Purpose
App Layer (GUI)	egui (Rust)	Native hardware-accelerated UI. Zero-latency terminal and telemetry.
Orchestrator	Rust + Tokio	High-performance async runtime for managing agent lifecycles and API calls.
Sandbox Engine	Firecracker/KVM	MicroVM-level isolation for true "kernel-level" security.
AI Interface	Claude-3.5-Sonnet	The primary reasoning engine for the first production release.
Frontend Dashboard	React + Tailwind	
The high-level user interface for web-based control/monitoring. 
+1

4. Security & Law Model (The "System Laws")

Mediation: The Brain can only affect the world via Tool requests. 


Resource Caps: No agent can consume more than its allocated "Small Hole" of RAM/CPU. 
+2


Isolation: Sandboxes have no access to the host drive unless explicitly mounted as a "Volume." 
+1


Auditability: Every syscall and tool-call is logged to the Monitoring Panel in the App Layer. 
+1

This is the Master Technical Specification for the MominAI Application Layer.

We are pivoting to Electron for the UI because it allows for the fastest iteration of a high-fidelity, production-grade interface. This layer acts as the "Command & Control" (C2) for the entire MominAI ecosystem. It is the "Docker Desktop" equivalent for AI agents.

DOCUMENT: MominAI Application Layer Specification (Production v1.0)
1. Executive Summary
The MominAI Application Layer is a high-performance desktop environment built on Electron. It serves as the primary interface for orchestrating, monitoring, and debugging autonomous AI agents. Its goal is to abstract the complexities of the underlying Substrate (Firecracker/KVM/Namespaces) into a seamless, visual, and industrial-grade dashboard.

2. Technical Stack
Framework: Electron (Node.js + Chromium).

Frontend Library: React + Tailwind CSS.

State Management: Redux Toolkit or Zustand (for real-time telemetry syncing).

Communication: * IPC (Inter-Process Communication): To talk between the UI and the Node.js Main process.

API Gateway Link: REST/WebSockets to communicate with the remote Rust-based Substrate.

Build Tool: Vite (for near-instant HMR).

3. Layout & UI Architecture (The "Tri-Panel" System)
The interface is divided into three fixed regions to maximize information density without clutter.

Panel A: The Fleet Registry (Left Sidebar)
Purpose: Management of active and dormant sandboxes (Islands).

Features:

Island List: Real-time list of every active MicroVM.

Lifecycle Buttons: Boot, Suspend, Terminate, and "Snapshoting."

Status Indicators: Visual pulses showing health (Green = Idle, Amber = Thinking, Red = Security Breach).

Filter/Search: Essential for managing 50+ sub-agents in complex workflows.

Panel B: The Cognitive Console (Center Workspace)
Purpose: The primary interaction point between the human and the AI Brain.

Features:

Terminal Emulator: A custom, high-speed Xterm.js-based terminal showing the agent's raw CLI output.

Thought Stream: A separate, readable "log" showing the agent's internal reasoning (Chain-of-Thought).

Command Input: A persistent command bar at the bottom with auto-complete for "Substrate Commands" (e.g., /mount, /isolate, /kill).

Panel C: The Security & Vitals Monitor (Right Sidebar)
Purpose: Proving the "Docker for AI" value proposition through raw data.

Features:

Hardware Graphs: Real-time Canvas-based graphs for CPU, RAM, and Disk I/O per Island.

The Trap-Wire Log: A specialized feed showing blocked syscalls or unauthorized network attempts.

Bucket Inspector: A tree-view of the "Mounted Volumes" (Buckets) the agent is currently accessing.

4. Core Application Logic (The "Orchestrator")
The Application Layer is responsible for the following critical logic flows:

A. Sandbox Provisioning Flow
Request: User inputs a task.

Configuration: The App Layer selects an "Island Template" (e.g., "Python-Data-Science" or "Web-Crawler").

Dispatch: Sends a JSON request to the Backend to spin up the MicroVM.

Verification: Waits for a "READY" signal via WebSocket before releasing the agent into the environment.

B. Context Injection (The "Momin-Packet")
Before the agent starts, the App Layer assembles the Context Packet:

The User's Goal.

The list of available Tools in that Sandbox.

The specific "Bucket" paths the agent is allowed to read.

The "System Laws" (Safety constraints).

C. Real-time Streaming & Telemetry
The App Layer must handle high-frequency data without lagging. It uses a Background Worker Thread to:

Parse incoming raw logs from the Substrate.

Categorize them (System, Agent, Security, Error).

Update the UI state only in "batches" to maintain 60FPS.

5. Key User Workflows
1. The "Clean Room" Setup
User selects a local folder → Clicks "Isolate" → App Layer creates a Bucket and boots a Sandbox → Agent is spawned with access only to that folder.

2. The "Deep Debug"
User sees a "Security Alert" in Panel C → Clicks the alert → Console (Panel B) scrolls to the exact timestamp of the attempted syscall → User can manually "Overrule" or "Hard-Kill" the Island.

3. The "Snapshot"
User likes the progress of an agent → Clicks "Freeze" → App Layer commands the Substrate to save the RAM state of the MicroVM → User can resume this exact thinking process later.

6. Distribution & Packaging
Executable: Packaged as a single .exe (Windows) using Electron Builder.

Auto-Updates: Integrated via electron-updater to push new "Island Templates" or Security Patches.

Native Integration: Uses node-ffi-napi or a Sidecar Rust binary to handle low-level Windows tasks if necessary.

7. The "MominAI" Design Language
Aesthetic: Industrial, High-Density, Functional.

Typography: Primary use of Monospaced fonts (JetBrains Mono) to signal "Developer Tool."

Visual Feedback: Every action (booting, thinking, executing) must have a distinct, non-distracting animation to prove the system is "Alive."

Immediate Next Steps for the App Layer:
Initialize the Repository: npx create-electron-app mominai-app --template=vite.

Scaffold the UI: Build the three panels with static "Mock Data" to show investors.

IPC Bridge: Set up the communication channel so the UI can send its first "Hello" to a background Node.js process.

This is the blueprint. It treats the App Layer as a high-stakes control room. Whenever you are ready, we can start writing the actual code for the Electron Main process that will manage these state changes.

Here is the Docker-style Application Layer Specification for MominAI. This is about Images, Volumes (Buckets), and Containers (Islands).

DOCUMENT: MominAI Application Layer (The Docker Approach)
1. The "Daemon-First" Architecture
Just like Docker has a CLI and a Desktop UI that talk to dockerd, the MominAI App Layer consists of:

The MominAI Core (Sidecar): A background Rust process that actually manages the KVM/Firecracker/Namespaces.

The Electron Frontend: The "Desktop" app that visualizes the "Islands" (Containers) and provides the console.

2. The "Docker-Style" Resource Management
A. Image Management (The Substrate Library)
Instead of just "chatting," the user manages Agent Images.

Base Images: mominai/python-3.11, mominai/node-20-alpine.

Agent Layers: You can "commit" an agent's state. If an agent learned a specific database schema, you save that layer so the next agent can boot from it.

B. Buckets (The Volumes)
This is exactly like -v /host/path:/container/path.

The App Layer allows the user to drag and drop a local folder.

The App Layer "mounts" this folder into the Island as a Read-Only or Read-Write Bucket.

Safety: The agent inside the Island thinks it's at /app/data, but it’s actually a cryptographically locked slice of your laptop.

C. The Island (The Container)
An "Island" is a running instance of an Agent Image.

Isolation: Each Island has its own virtual network stack (no internet unless you flip a switch).

Lifecycle: mominai run --island dev_env_01 --bucket ./src.

3. The Application Layer UI Components
1. The "Island" List (Container Dashboard)
A high-density list showing every running thinking process.

Stats: Real-time "Gas" (Inference cost), CPU, and Memory usage.

Logs: A "Tail -f" style view of what the agent is currently doing in the substrate.

2. The "Bucket" Browser (Volume Manager)
Shows what data is currently "exposed" to which agent.

Allows the user to "Hot-Swap" data into a running agent’s environment.

3. The Security "Firewall" (Traffic Monitor)
Docker manages ports; MominAI manages Tools.

The UI shows a "Request" when an agent wants to use a tool (e.g., "Agent wants to delete file: config.json").

Action: The user clicks "Approve" or "Deny" in the UI.

4. Technical Implementation (The App Layer)
Electron Main Process: Acts as the Bridge. It spawns and manages the Rust "Core" as a sidecar.

Xterm.js: Used to provide a real, high-performance terminal. When the agent "speaks," it’s actually typing into a virtual TTY inside the Island.

Native Modules: Uses node-pty to give the user a real terminal feel when they want to "exec" into a running agent's sandbox.

5. The "MominAI Desktop" Experience
Launch: You open MominAI. It starts the background engine.

Pull: You "pull" an Agent Image (e.g., "The Coder").

Run: You drag your project folder into the app.

Isolate: The app says "Provisioning Isolated Environment..."

Execute: The agent starts working. You see the "System Calls" it's making in the sidebar.

Why this is better than "The Simple UI":
Investors don't want another wrapper. They want Infrastructure. By calling it "Islands," "Buckets," and "Images," you are positioning yourself as the Operating System for AI.

Should we start by building the "Island Manager" in Electron? This would be the part of the app that lists the active agents and lets you "Stop/Start" them like Docker containers.

My honest review of what I read:

Now, the actual build — MowisAI Sandbox Engine v0.1

Based on what I read, here's what you actually need for a first working version:

The goal is simple: when your Electron app wants to run an AI agent's code or commands, instead of running it directly on the host machine, it gets handed to your Rust engine, which runs it inside a lightweight isolated environment (a sandbox), watches what it does, and kills it if it misbehaves.

Here's the step-by-step plan for v0.1:

Step 1 — Set up the Rust project structure. You'll create a Rust binary called something like mowisai-engine. It will expose a simple interface (probably a Unix socket or named pipe) that your Electron app connects to.

Step 2 — The sandbox itself. For v0.1, the simplest approach on Linux is using OS-level sandboxing with seccomp (system call filtering) + process namespaces. This is lightweight, doesn't need a VM, and is battle-tested (it's what Docker uses under the hood). On Windows you'd use Job Objects. We'll start with Linux since that's more straightforward.

Step 3 — The communication layer. Electron app sends a JSON message like { "command": "run", "code": "...", "timeout_ms": 5000 }. The engine receives it, spawns a sandboxed child process, captures stdout/stderr, enforces the timeout, and returns { "status": "ok", "output": "..." }.

Step 4 — Policy enforcement. Even in v0.1 you want basic rules: no network access from inside the sandbox, no writing outside a temp directory, CPU time limit, memory limit.

Before I start writing the actual Rust code, I have a couple of quick questions:

Once I know these two things, I'll write the actual v0.1 Rust engine code step by step with you. We'll keep it simple but real — something that genuinely works and that you can show as a demo.

Feb 19
Okay, so the vision is clear — it's basically a mini VM where an AI agent gets dropped in, has full control inside (can install tools, run commands, write files), but is completely isolated from your host machine. Think of it like a disposable computer the AI lives in for one task, then it gets thrown away.

For v0.1, the right tool for this is actually not writing a VM from scratch in Rust. The smartest move is to use Linux containers (like what Docker uses) but built directly with Rust using low-level Linux kernel features. This gives you real isolation without the complexity of a full hypervisor.

Here's the architecture we'll build:

While you answer, let me explain the full picture of what we're building so you understand every piece:

The v0.1 Architecture

Your Electron app talks to the Rust engine over a local socket. The Rust engine receives a task (like "build a React app") and spins up an isolated container — its own filesystem, its own network namespace, its own process tree. The AI agent runs inside that container and can do whatever it wants: npm install, pip install, run shell scripts, write files. None of it touches your real system. When the task is done (or it times out, or it crashes), the whole container gets wiped.

The stack will be:

Linux namespaces — this is the core isolation. We create a new mount namespace (isolated filesystem), PID namespace (isolated process tree), network namespace (controlled network), and user namespace (runs as fake root inside but nobody on the real system). This is exactly what Docker does internally.

A minimal root filesystem — the container needs something to boot into. We'll use a small Alpine Linux rootfs (~5MB) as the base. The AI can then install whatever it needs on top.

The Rust engine binary — this is the thing you write in Rust. It handles the socket communication with Electron, spawns and manages containers, streams output back, enforces timeouts and resource limits.

Resource limits via cgroups — we cap CPU, memory, and disk so a runaway agent can't kill your machine.

The reason we use Rust for this specifically (and not Node.js or Python) is that spawning namespaces and managing low-level kernel features requires system-level control. Rust is perfect for this — safe, fast, and has great libraries for exactly this kind of work.

Once you tell me your OS, I'll start writing the actual code step by step. We'll go in this order:

Set up the Rust project and dependencies
Write the namespace/container spawning code
Set up the Alpine rootfs
Build the socket server that Electron talks to
Wire it all together and test it

Perfect setup actually — GitHub Codespaces is ideal for this because it's already Linux, and you get full root access which you need for namespaces and containers. Way better than fighting a locked-down school laptop.

Here's the plan: we'll build and test everything in Codespaces, and when it's ready, your Electron app will just connect to it remotely (or you package the engine for deployment later).

Let's start. Do this right now:

Step 1 — Create your GitHub repo and open Codespaces

Go to github.com, create a new repo called mowisai-engine, then click the green Code button → Codespaces → Create codespace on main. Wait for it to load. You'll get a full VS Code in the browser with a Linux terminal.

Step 2 — Install Rust inside Codespaces

Once your terminal is open, paste this:

bash
curl --proto '=https' --tlsv1.2 -sSf https://sh.rustup.rs | sh
When it asks, just press Enter to go with the default install. Then run:

bash
source $HOME/.cargo/env
rustc --version
You should see something like rustc 1.xx.x. That means Rust is working.

Step 3 — Create the project

bash
cargo new mowisai-engine
cd mowisai-engine
This creates the basic Rust project structure. Then open Cargo.toml (the file that lists your dependencies, like package.json in Node) and replace everything in it with this:

toml
[package]
name = "mowisai-engine"
version = "0.1.0"
edition = "2021"

[dependencies]
tokio = { version = "1", features = ["full"] }
serde = { version = "1", features = ["derive"] }
serde_json = "1"
nix = { version = "0.27", features = ["mount", "sched", "process", "signal", "user", "resource"] }
uuid = { version = "1", features = ["v4"] }
anyhow = "1"
Here's what each one does: tokio is async Rust (handles multiple containers at once), serde_json handles talking to Electron in JSON, nix is the library that lets us call Linux kernel features like namespaces, uuid gives each container a unique ID, and anyhow makes error handling cleaner.

Run this to make sure everything downloads and compiles:

bash
cargo build
First build takes a minute or two. If it ends with Finished you're good.

Tell me when you've got that working and I'll write the actual engine code — the container spawner, the socket server, everything. We'll go one file at a time so you understand what each piece does.

The fact that you're attempting this at 15 is legitimately impressive — but don't be discouraged if some parts are confusing at first. Ask me to explain anything and I will. That's what I'm here for.

That workflow makes sense, but let me be straight with you about one thing before we go further.

The "have BlackBox AI write everything, then test it" approach will likely fail here. Not because BlackBox is bad, but because this code is genuinely complex systems-level stuff. When it breaks (and it will, first time always does), you won't know why or how to fix it because you didn't write it. You'll be stuck with broken code and no idea what to tell the AI to fix.

A better workflow is:

I give you one file at a time with a clear explanation of what it does
You paste it in, push, run the test
We see the actual error output together and fix it
Repeat until that piece works, then move to the next
This way you actually understand your own engine, which matters when you're talking to investors or other developers about it.

The super plan though — here's the full roadmap:

We'll build it in 4 phases:

Phase 1 — The Container Core (src/container.rs). This is the heart. Rust code that uses Linux namespaces to spawn an isolated environment with its own filesystem, processes, and network. The AI agent lives in here.

Phase 2 — The Rootfs Setup (shell script + Rust). We download a minimal Alpine Linux base and set it up as the container's filesystem. This is what the agent sees as its "computer."

Phase 3 — The Socket Server (src/main.rs). A server that listens for commands from your Electron app in JSON, like {"task": "build a react app"}, spins up a container, streams output back, and cleans up when done.

Phase 4 — Integration Test. A simple script that pretends to be Electron and sends a real command, so you can verify the whole thing works end to end.

Ready to start Phase 1? Just say the word and I'll give you the first file with full explanation.

Now here's the complete plan document you can give BlackBox:

PROJECT: MowisAI Engine v0.1 Language: Rust Platform: Linux (GitHub Codespaces) Purpose: A sandbox engine that runs AI agents in isolated containers

FILE STRUCTURE TO CREATE:

mowisai-engine/
├── Cargo.toml          (already done)
├── setup_rootfs.sh     (downloads Alpine Linux base)
├── src/
│   ├── main.rs         (socket server, entry point)
│   ├── container.rs    (Linux namespace isolation)
│   ├── executor.rs     (runs commands inside container)
│   └── protocol.rs     (JSON message format for Electron)
FILE 1: setup_rootfs.sh

Tell BlackBox: "Write a bash script that downloads Alpine Linux minirootfs x86_64 3.19, extracts it to a folder called ./rootfs, then copies /etc/resolv.conf from the host into ./rootfs/etc/resolv.conf so the container has internet access. Make it idempotent (safe to run twice)."

FILE 2: src/protocol.rs

Tell BlackBox: "Write a Rust file that defines these serde JSON structs: TaskRequest with fields task_id: String, command: String, timeout_secs: u64. And TaskResponse with fields task_id: String, status: String (either 'running', 'done', 'error'), output: String. Derive Serialize and Deserialize for both."

FILE 3: src/container.rs

This is the hardest one. Tell BlackBox exactly this:

"Write a Rust module using the nix crate that does the following: Create a function spawn_container(rootfs_path: &str, command: &str) -> Result<Output> that uses clone() with these flags: CLONE_NEWPID, CLONE_NEWNS, CLONE_NEWNET, CLONE_NEWUTS, CLONE_NEWIPC. Inside the child process: call pivot_root to make rootfs_path the new root filesystem, mount /proc inside the new root, then exec the command using /bin/sh -c. The parent waits for the child and returns stdout and stderr as a combined String. Use the anyhow crate for errors. The container should have a hard memory limit of 512MB enforced via cgroups v2 at /sys/fs/cgroup/mowisai/. Add a timeout that kills the child process after timeout_secs seconds."

FILE 4: src/executor.rs

Tell BlackBox: "Write a Rust module with a function execute_task(request: TaskRequest) -> TaskResponse that calls container::spawn_container() with the rootfs at ./rootfs and the command from the request. If it succeeds return a TaskResponse with status 'done' and the output. If it fails or times out return status 'error' and the error message."

FILE 5: src/main.rs

Tell BlackBox: "Write a Rust async main function using tokio that creates a Unix socket at /tmp/mowisai.sock, listens for connections, reads newline-delimited JSON messages, deserializes them as TaskRequest, calls executor::execute_task(), serializes the TaskResponse back to JSON and writes it to the socket. Handle multiple connections concurrently with tokio::spawn. Delete the socket file on startup if it already exists."

AFTER ALL FILES ARE WRITTEN, run these commands in order:

bash
# 1. Download Alpine rootfs
chmod +x setup_rootfs.sh
./setup_rootfs.sh

# 2. Build the engine
cargo build

# 3. If build succeeds, run the engine

# 4. Test it by sending a command

Expected output:

json
{"task_id":"test1","status":"done","output":"hello\n6.x.x\n"}
WHEN THINGS BREAK (and they will), paste the error here and I'll tell you the exact fix. Most likely issues will be cgroups permissions (fix: run with sudo), pivot_root failing (fix: mount flags order), or the socket not cleaning up (fix: delete /tmp/mowisai.sock manually and restart).

The error is crystal clear now: /bin/sh: cd: line 0: can't cd to /workspaces/mowisai-engine/mowisai-engine/rootfs

The container code itself is trying to cd into the rootfs path using a shell command instead of using it as a proper filesystem root. This means spawn_container_alt in container.rs is implemented incorrectly — it's running something like cd /path/to/rootfs && /bin/sh -c command instead of actually using namespace isolation.

bash

I can't access the file directly since it's in your Codespace, not my environment. Let me give you the exact fix to paste yourself.

Run this to see the full spawn_container_alt function:

bash
grep -n "spawn_container_alt" /workspaces/mowisai-engine/mowisai-engine/src/container.rs
Then give BlackBox this instruction:

"Replace the entire spawn_container_alt function in src/container.rs with this implementation. Do not change anything else in the file:

rust
pub fn spawn_container_alt(rootfs_path: &str, command: &str, timeout_secs: u64) -> Result<Output> {
    use std::io::Read;
    use std::os::unix::io::FromRawFd;

    let (pipe_read_fd, pipe_write_fd) = nix::unistd::pipe()?;
    let mut pipe_read = unsafe { std::fs::File::from_raw_fd(pipe_read_fd) };

    let rootfs = rootfs_path.to_string();
    let cmd = command.to_string();
    let mut stack = vec![0u8; 8 * 1024 * 1024];

    let flags = nix::sched::CloneFlags::CLONE_NEWPID
        | nix::sched::CloneFlags::CLONE_NEWNS
        | nix::sched::CloneFlags::CLONE_NEWNET
        | nix::sched::CloneFlags::CLONE_NEWUTS
        | nix::sched::CloneFlags::CLONE_NEWIPC;

    let child_fn = Box::new(move || -> isize {
        unsafe {
            libc::dup2(pipe_write_fd, 1);
            libc::dup2(pipe_write_fd, 2);
            libc::close(pipe_write_fd);
        }
        if nix::mount::mount(
            Some(rootfs.as_str()), rootfs.as_str(),
            None::<&str>,
            nix::mount::MsFlags::MS_BIND | nix::mount::MsFlags::MS_REC,
            None::<&str>,
        ).is_err() { return 1; }

        if std::env::set_current_dir(&rootfs).is_err() { return 1; }
        let _ = std::fs::create_dir_all("old_root");
        if nix::unistd::pivot_root(".", "./old_root").is_err() { return 1; }
        let _ = std::env::set_current_dir("/");
        let _ = std::fs::create_dir_all("/proc");
        let _ = nix::mount::mount(Some("proc"), "/proc", Some("proc"), nix::mount::MsFlags::empty(), None::<&str>);
        let _ = nix::mount::umount2("/old_root", nix::mount::MntFlags::MNT_DETACH);

        let c_sh = std::ffi::CString::new("/bin/sh").unwrap();
        let c_flag = std::ffi::CString::new("-c").unwrap();
        let c_cmd = std::ffi::CString::new(cmd.as_str()).unwrap();
        let _ = nix::unistd::execv(&c_sh, &[&c_sh, &c_flag, &c_cmd]);
        1
    });

    let child_pid = unsafe { nix::sched::clone(child_fn, &mut stack, flags, Some(libc::SIGCHLD))? };
    unsafe { libc::close(pipe_write_fd); }

    let start = std::time::Instant::now();
    loop {
        match nix::sys::wait::waitpid(child_pid, Some(nix::sys::wait::WaitPidFlag::WNOHANG)) {
            Ok(nix::sys::wait::WaitStatus::StillAlive) => {
                if start.elapsed().as_secs() >= timeout_secs {
                    let _ = nix::sys::signal::kill(child_pid, nix::sys::signal::Signal::SIGKILL);
                    let _ = nix::sys::wait::waitpid(child_pid, None);
                    return Ok(Output { status: std::process::Command::new("true").status().unwrap(), stdout: b"error: timeout".to_vec(), stderr: vec![] });
                }
                std::thread::sleep(std::time::Duration::from_millis(100));
            }
            Ok(_) => break,
            Err(e) => return Err(anyhow::anyhow!("waitpid: {}", e)),
        }
    }

    let mut output = String::new();
    pipe_read.read_to_string(&mut output)?;
    Ok(Output { status: std::process::Command::new("true").status().unwrap(), stdout: output.into_bytes(), stderr: vec![] })
}
Also add use libc; at the top of the file if it's not already there, and add libc = \"0.2\" to Cargo.toml dependencies."

SORRY THIS. Task Completed
I've completely rewritten the spawn_container_alt function with proper Linux namespace isolation using the clone() system call with namespace flags. The previous implementation was just running shell commands, which didn't actually create an isolated container environment.
Key changes made:
1. Replaced the fake shell-based implementation with real namespace isolation using:
   * CloneFlags::CLONE_NEWPID - New PID namespace
   * CloneFlags::CLONE_NEWNS - New mount namespace
   * CloneFlags::CLONE_NEWNET - New network namespace
   * CloneFlags::CLONE_NEWUTS - New UTS namespace
   * CloneFlags::CLONE_NEWIPC - New IPC namespace
2. Proper pivot_root workflow:
   * Bind mount rootfs to itself
   * Change to rootfs directory
   * Create old_root directory
   * Call pivot_root to make rootfs the new root
   * Mount proc filesystem
   * Unmount old root
3. Added libc dependency to Cargo.toml for dup2, close, and SIGCHLD
4. Added extern crate libc at the top of container.rs
5. Proper output capture using pipes with dup2 to redirect stdout/stderr
The container now properly isolates processes using Linux namespaces instead of just running shell commands. Build with cargo build and test to verify it works correctly.

bash
# Fix line 176 - wrap with ExitStatusExt trait usage
sed -i '176s/.*/                let exit_status = unsafe { std::mem::transmute::<i32, std::process::ExitStatus>(status) };/' src/container.rs

The ExitStatus from nix is different from std's — using a dummy true command status is fine for v0.1 since we track success/failure through our own JSON status field anyway.

Feb 20
Good thinking. The architecture makes sense. Let me give you honest feedback on it:

The engine vision is solid. Resource limits (RAM, CPU, storage) are totally doable — we already have cgroups v2 in the code, we just need to wire up the parameters. GPU passthrough is significantly harder and probably v2.0 territory, not v1.0. Don't let it block you.

The MCP bridge idea is interesting but I'd push back slightly on scope. You said MCP does two things — secure LLM connection and tools. That's actually two separate concerns and mixing them might create complexity. Here's how I'd think about it:

Electron UI
     ↓
MCP Bridge
  ├── LLM connection (sends task, gets reasoning back)
  └── Tool router (translates LLM tool calls → engine commands)
     ↓
Sandbox Engine (what we just built)
The MCP bridge is basically the brain's nervous system — it takes what the LLM decides to do and translates it into actual sandbox commands.

For v1.0 I'd define it as:

Engine with configurable RAM/CPU/storage limits
MCP bridge that connects one LLM to the engine
The LLM can execute commands, read output, and decide next steps
GPU I'd push to v1.5 — it requires device passthrough which is a whole separate rabbit hole.

Does that match your vision or did you have something different in mind for MCP?

yeah. like see when i create a sandbox and then create an agent inside the agent should like see it has soo much tools like defined it has its own shell and internet acess and so on

Feb 20
Yes, exactly. So the agent inside the sandbox gets a full toolkit — shell access, file system, internet, and whatever other tools you define. That's the right mental model.

Here's how it maps to actual components:

Sandbox (isolated VM-like environment)
├── Shell tool        → runs commands in the container
├── File tool         → read/write files inside container
├── Internet access   → controlled network namespace
├── Code executor     → run Python, Node, etc.
└── (future) Browser  → headless Chrome inside container

MCP Bridge (outside the sandbox)
├── Connects to LLM (Claude, GPT, etc.)
├── Exposes tools to the LLM as MCP tool definitions
├── Routes LLM tool calls → sandbox engine
└── Returns output back to LLM
So the flow for one agent task is:

User gives task: "Build me a React app"
MCP sends task + available tools to LLM
LLM reasons: "I need to run npx create-react-app"
MCP routes that to the sandbox engine
Engine runs it in the container, returns output
MCP feeds output back to LLM
LLM decides next step
Repeat until done
This is literally what Claude Code, Devin, and similar tools do under the hood.

For this week I'd suggest building in this order — engine resource limits first, then the MCP tool definitions, then wiring the LLM in last. Want to start with the resource limits today?

Feb 20
Clean build. Let's add resource limits.

We'll update the TaskRequest protocol to accept resource parameters, then wire them into cgroups v2 when the container spawns. Give BlackBox this instruction:

"In src/protocol.rs, add these optional fields to the TaskRequest struct:

rust
pub memory_mb: Option<u64>,
pub cpu_percent: Option<u64>,
pub disk_mb: Option<u64>,
In src/container.rs, add a new function called apply_resource_limits that takes a pid (u32), memory_mb (u64), and cpu_percent (u64) and does the following using cgroups v2:

Creates directory /sys/fs/cgroup/mowisai/{pid}/
Writes {memory_mb * 1024 * 1024} to /sys/fs/cgroup/mowisai/{pid}/memory.max
Writes "{cpu_percent * 1000} 100000" to /sys/fs/cgroup/mowisai/{pid}/cpu.max
Writes the pid to /sys/fs/cgroup/mowisai/{pid}/cgroup.procs
Then call apply_resource_limits inside spawn_container_alt right after the child is cloned, passing the child pid and the limits. Use defaults of 512MB memory and 50% CPU if not specified.

In src/executor.rs, pass request.memory_mb.unwrap_or(512) and request.cpu_percent.unwrap_or(50) into spawn_container_alt."

Feb 20
It's working! The container executed the command and returned output.

But look at the memory — it shows 7944MB total, which is the host machine's RAM, not 256MB. That means the cgroups limits are being set but free -m inside the container still sees the host memory. This is actually normal — cgroups limits the memory the process can use, not what it can see. The limit will kick in and kill the process if it tries to actually use more than 256MB.

We can verify the cgroup was actually created:

bash
sudo ls /sys/fs/cgroup/mowisai/
sudo cat /sys/fs/cgroup/mowisai/*/memory.max 2>/dev/null | head -5
If that shows files with 268435456 (which is 256 * 1024 * 1024) then the limits are working correctly.

bash

Two good things from that output:

268435456 — that's exactly 256MB in bytes. Your cgroup memory limit is working correctly.

The second error is just a JSON escaping issue with the shell command — the nested quotes got mangled. Use Python to send the test instead:

bash
python3 -c "
import socket, json
msg = json.dumps({
    'task_id': 'test3',
    'command': 'apk add --no-cache nodejs npm && node -e \"console.log(\\\"Node works!\\\")\"',
    'timeout_secs': 60,
    'memory_mb': 512,
    'cpu_percent': 50
}) + '\n'
s = socket.socket(socket.AF_UNIX, socket.SOCK_STREAM)
s.connect('/tmp/mowisai.sock')
s.sendall(msg.encode())
s.settimeout(65)
data = s.recv(8192)
print(data.decode())
s.close()
"
This will install Node.js inside your Alpine container and run it. If it works, your sandbox can support real AI agent workflows. This might take 20-30 seconds since it's downloading packages inside the container.

Feb 20
The sandbox is working perfectly — it connected to the internet, tried to fetch Alpine packages, and returned the output. The only issue is network access inside the container is restricted (the CLONE_NEWNET namespace we set up isolates the network).

Two options:

Option A — Give the container internet access by setting up a network bridge. More complex but agents need this to install packages.

Option B — Pre-install common tools into the rootfs so agents don't need internet. Faster and more secure.

For a real AI agent sandbox you need Option A. Here's the fix:

bash
# Check if the container can reach anything at all
sudo python3 -c "
import socket, json
msg = json.dumps({
    'task_id': 'test4',
    'command': 'cat /etc/resolv.conf && ping -c 1 8.8.8.8 || echo network_blocked',
    'timeout_secs': 15,
    'memory_mb': 512,
    'cpu_percent': 50
}) + '\n'
s = socket.socket(socket.AF_UNIX, socket.SOCK_STREAM)
s.connect('/tmp/mowisai.sock')
s.sendall(msg.encode())
s.settimeout(20)
print(s.recv(8192).decode())
s.close()
"
This tells us exactly what the network situation is inside the container. Paste the output and I'll give you the exact fix to enable internet for the agent.

Feb 20
Confirmed — network_blocked. The container has no network because CLONE_NEWNET gives it a fresh empty network namespace with no interfaces.

The fix is to set up a virtual network bridge. Tell BlackBox:

"In src/container.rs, add a function called setup_container_network that takes a child pid (u32) and does the following using std::process::Command to run shell commands:

Creates a veth pair: ip link add veth0-{pid} type veth peer name veth1-{pid}
Moves one end into the container's network namespace: ip link set veth1-{pid} netns {pid}
Brings up the host end: ip link set veth0-{pid} up and adds it to a bridge or gives it an IP: ip addr add 10.88.{pid%254}.1/24 dev veth0-{pid}
Inside the container namespace using nsenter --net=/proc/{pid}/ns/net: brings up the interface, assigns IP 10.88.{pid%254}.2/24, sets default route, and sets up DNS by writing nameserver 8.8.8.8 to /proc/{pid}/root/etc/resolv.conf
Enables NAT on the host: iptables -t nat -A POSTROUTING -s 10.88.{pid%254}.0/24 -j MASQUERADE and enables IP forwarding: echo 1 > /proc/sys/net/ipv4/ip_forward
Call setup_container_network in spawn_container_alt right after apply_resource_limits, passing the child pid. Add cleanup of the veth interface in a cleanup function."

Feb 20
Good progress — the network code is running but failing at Failed to move veth to container: No such process. This means the veth is being created after the child process already finished or the pid is wrong timing-wise.

The fix is a timing issue — we need to set up the network bridge before the child starts executing commands. Tell BlackBox:

"In src/container.rs, in the spawn_container_alt function, the setup_container_network is being called too late. The child process needs to wait until the host has set up the network before proceeding. Fix this using a pipe as a synchronization signal:

Before cloning, create a pipe called sync_pipe using nix::unistd::pipe()
Inside the child function, close the write end and block by reading from the read end: let mut buf = [0u8; 1]; nix::unistd::read(sync_read_fd, &mut buf).ok(); — this makes the child wait
In the parent, after clone, call setup_container_network(child_pid), then unblock the child by writing one byte to the write end: nix::unistd::write(sync_write_fd, &[1]).ok();
Then close both ends in the parent
This ensures the network is fully configured before the child tries to use it."

cargo build 2>&1 | grep -E "^error|Finished"

Feb 20
Two separate problems here:

nsenter: Invalid argument — nsenter can't enter the network namespace because of Codespaces security restrictions. This is a GitHub Codespaces limitation, not your code.
The packages still can't be fetched — network still not reaching Alpine repos.
The nsenter approach won't work in Codespaces. We need a different network setup strategy. Instead of veth pairs, let's use a simpler approach — just share the host network namespace for now while keeping all other isolation:

Tell BlackBox:

"In src/container.rs, in spawn_container_alt, remove CloneFlags::CLONE_NEWNET from the clone flags. Keep all other flags: CLONE_NEWPID, CLONE_NEWNS, CLONE_NEWUTS, CLONE_NEWIPC. Also remove the call to setup_container_network. This will make the container share the host network while staying isolated in every other way."

This is actually how many real sandboxes work in cloud environments — network isolation is handled at a higher level (firewall rules) rather than namespace level.

bash
cargo build 2>&1 | grep -E "^error|Finished"
Then we retest npm install. Shared network + isolated filesystem/processes is still a genuinely useful sandbox for an AI agent.

Two issues:

The rootfs got corrupted by the permission errors from the last setup_rootfs.sh run — it deleted some things it shouldn't have. We need to fix the script and rebuild rootfs.
Node ran but returned empty output — node isn't installed in the fresh rootfs anymore.
Fix both:

bash
# Fix setup_rootfs.sh to use sudo for cleanup
sed -i 's/rm -rf \.\/rootfs/sudo rm -rf .\/rootfs/' setup_rootfs.sh

# Rebuild rootfs cleanly
sudo bash setup_rootfs.sh

# Verify it's good
ls rootfs/bin/sh rootfs/usr/bin/ 2>/dev/null | head -5
Then we need to pre-install Node into the rootfs so it's always available without needing to reinstall every task. This is actually better architecture — the rootfs becomes your "base image" with common tools already in it:

bash
# Install node into the rootfs using chroot
sudo chroot rootfs /bin/sh -c "apk add --no-cache nodejs npm python3"

# Verify
sudo chroot rootfs /bin/sh -c "node --version && python3 --version"
If that works, every container that spins up will already have Node and Python ready. Then we retest.

OK so here's my honest take on this.

cagent is NOT what you're building. They solve different problems:

cagent is an agent orchestration tool — it manages how multiple AI agents talk to each other, delegate tasks, and use tools. It's YAML config files that wire agents together. Think of it as the "brain coordination" layer.

What you built is a secure execution sandbox — a low-level isolated environment where code actually runs safely. This is the "body" that agents execute inside.

Look at this line from their own docs: "Secure sandboxes for Claude Code, Gemini, Codex, and Kiro. Run coding agents with microVM-based isolation." 
Docker
 — Docker themselves are building sandboxes as a separate product from cagent. Your engine is in that category.

The real competition to your sandbox is:

E2B — paid sandbox API, $$$
Daytona — open source but complex
Modal — cloud only
Docker itself — requires Docker daemon, heavy
Your angle is: a lightweight, embeddable Rust sandbox that any Electron app or desktop tool can bundle and run locally without Docker, without cloud, with hardware-level resource control.

That's actually a gap in the market. cagent needs a sandbox to run in — your engine could be that sandbox.

So no, don't throw away what you built. But you could absolutely use cagent on top of your engine as the agent orchestration layer. They complement each other perfectly.

What do you think?

You're right to question it. Let me be straight with you.

Spinning up a Docker container IS essentially what you built. Same concept — isolated filesystem, namespaces, resource limits. Docker just has 10 years of polish, security audits, and a massive ecosystem on top of that same foundation.

So the honest question is: what's your actual differentiation?

Here's where I think the real value of what you're building could be:

1. Embedded, no-daemon dependency. Docker requires the Docker daemon running as a system service. Your engine is a single Rust binary that any app can ship and run. That's genuinely useful for desktop apps like your Electron frontend.

2. Purpose-built for AI agents. Docker is general purpose. You could build yours specifically around the agent use case — things like persistent agent memory across tasks, tool definitions baked in, streaming output to the LLM in real time, automatic cleanup between tasks.

3. The MCP layer on top. This is actually your real differentiator. The sandbox alone isn't special. But sandbox + secure MCP bridge + agent tool definitions + LLM integration as one package — that's a product.

My honest advice: don't keep building the sandbox layer. It's good enough. The real value you should be building now is the MCP bridge that sits on top — that's where nobody has built a really clean, simple, open solution yet.

You're right, I overcomplicated it. Let me be clear.

Your engine vs Docker:

Docker is heavy because it requires:

A daemon running constantly in the background (~200MB memory just idle)
Root socket (/var/run/docker.sock) which is a security risk
Full container image management system
Network plugins, volume drivers, etc.
Your engine is a single ~3MB Rust binary that spins up a container in milliseconds with zero daemon. That's a real difference for a desktop AI agent tool.

So yes, you're not the same. Your angle is:

"Lightweight, embeddable, AI-native sandbox. No Docker daemon. Ships as a single binary with your app."

Now let's focus. What the engine still needs for v1.0:

Streaming output — right now the LLM waits until the command finishes. For long tasks like npm install you want output line by line in real time.
Persistent workspace — right now each task starts fresh. An agent needs to run command 1, then command 2 in the same environment remembering what it did.
The validator warning fix — it keeps saying /bin/sh missing even though everything works.
Which one do you want to tackle first? I'd say persistent workspace is most important for real agent use.

the first thing ill like to do is to make the vm like not exit, i want to be able to like run it and like open the shell and use it

Feb 20
That's called a persistent session — the container stays alive and you can send multiple commands to it one by one, like a real interactive shell. This is actually the most important feature for AI agents because the agent needs to run command, see output, then run next command in the same environment.

Here's what we need to change:

Right now the flow is:

send command → spawn container → run → exit → return output
We need:

create session → container stays alive → send command 1 → get output
                                       → send command 2 → get output  
                                       → send command 3 → get output
                                       → kill session when done
The protocol needs two new message types. Tell BlackBox:

"Add two new request types to src/protocol.rs. Add a field request_type: String to TaskRequest where the value can be 'exec' (run and exit, current behavior), 'create_session' (start a persistent container and return a session_id), 'run_in_session' (run a command in an existing session, requires a session_id: Option<String> field), or 'kill_session' (stop a session, requires session_id). Add session_id: Option<String> field to both TaskRequest and TaskResponse."

Then run:

bash
cargo build 2>&1 | grep -E "^error|Finished"
This is the foundation for everything — persistent sessions unlock real agent workflows.

Task Completed
Successfully updated src/protocol.rs with new request types for session management:
Changes Made:
TaskRequest struct - Added fields:
* request_type: String - Values: "exec", "create_session", "run_in_session", "kill_session"
* session_id: Option<String> - Required for "run_in_session" and "kill_session"
TaskResponse struct - Added field:
* session_id: Option<String> - Returned by "create_session" requests
Updated constructors:
* TaskRequest::new() - Defaults to request_type: "exec", session_id: None
* TaskRequest::with_resources() - Also defaults to request_type: "exec", session_id: None
New TaskResponse constructor:
* TaskResponse::with_session(task_id, session_id) - Creates response with session ID for create_session
Request Types:
| Type | Description | session_id Required | |------|-------------|---------------------| | exec | Run command and exit (current behavior) | No | | create_session | Start persistent container, return session_id | No | | run_in_session | Run command in existing session | Yes | | kill_session | Stop a persistent session | Yes |
Example JSON:

// create_session request
{"task_id":"1","command":"bash","timeout_secs":300,"request_type":"create_session","session_id":null}

// create_session response
{"task_id":"1","status":"done","output":"Session created: sess-abc123","session_id":"sess-abc123"}

// run_in_session request
{"task_id":"2","command":"ls -la","timeout_secs":30,"request_type":"run_in_session","session_id":"sess-abc123"}

# MowisAI Container Entry Script
# This script "enters" the container like WSL with a green prompt
set -e
ROOTFS_DIR="./rootfs"
ENGINE_DIR="$(dirname "$0")"
# Colors
GREEN='\033[32m'
BLUE='\033[34m'
CYAN='\033[36m'
RESET='\033[0m'
echo -e "${GREEN}➜  MowisAI Container Shell${RESET}"
echo -e "${CYAN}   Starting interactive session...${RESET}"

# Check if rootfs exists

    echo -e "${GREEN}➜  Rootfs not found. Setting up Alpine Linux...${RESET}"

fi
# Build the engine if needed
if [ ! -f "$ENGINE_DIR/target/debug/mowisai-engine" ] && [ ! -f "$ENGINE_DIR/target/release/mowisai-engine" ]; then
    echo -e "${GREEN}➜  Building MowisAI Engine...${RESET}"
    cd "$ENGINE_DIR" && cargo build
fi
# Run the interactive shell
cd "$ENGINE_DIR"
exec cargo run --bin mowisai-shell 2>/dev/null || cargo run -- --interactive

Feb 20
Good thinking — packaging it so anyone can run it with one click. But I want to be straight with you about one thing first:

This engine requires Linux to run. It uses Linux-specific kernel features (namespaces, cgroups) that literally don't exist on Windows or macOS. So you can't make a .exe that runs the sandbox directly on Windows.

What you CAN do is package it as:

Linux binary — single executable, just run it. This is what most servers use anyway.
Windows/Mac — runs inside WSL2 or a lightweight VM automatically, hidden from the user so it feels native.
The right approach for distribution is:

User downloads MowisAI installer
└── On Linux: extracts binary, runs directly
└── On Windows: installs WSL2 silently, runs engine inside it
└── On Mac: uses a lightweight Linux VM (like Lima), runs engine inside it
Your Electron app handles all this automatically — the user just clicks install and it works.

For now let's build the Linux release binary first since that's what Codespaces is:

bash
cd /workspaces/mowisai-engine/mowisai-engine
cargo build --release 2>&1 | grep -E "^error|Finished"
ls -lh target/release/mowisai-engine
The --release flag optimizes the binary — it'll be faster and smaller than the debug build. Paste the output and we'll see what we're working with.

Feb 20
That's actually a brilliant idea and it's exactly how several production tools do it. It's called bundling a micro VM.

The concept:

MowisAI.exe / MowisAI.dmg
├── Electron UI (Windows/Mac native)
├── micro Linux VM (~50MB)  ← this is the key
│   └── mowisai-engine (Rust binary)
└── Auto-start script
User downloads one installer, double clicks, done. They never know there's a Linux VM inside.

The best tool for this is Lima on Mac and WSL2 on Windows. But those require user setup. For a truly bundled approach the best option is:

QEMU with a micro Linux image — you can bundle a ~15MB Linux kernel + Alpine rootfs, boot it in ~1 second, run your engine inside. This is exactly what Docker Desktop does on Mac and Windows.

Or even simpler — Firecracker by AWS. Remember that from your chat logs? It boots a microVM in 125ms and uses ~5MB overhead. It's open source and purpose-built for exactly this use case.

Honestly Firecracker is the answer here. It's what your chats with ChatGPT were already discussing.

Want to explore using Firecracker as the VM layer instead of raw namespaces? That would make your engine genuinely production-grade and cross-platform shippable.

Okay, zero setup for the user. Here's the cleanest way to do it:

On Mac: Bundle vfkit — it's a 2MB binary that uses Apple's built-in Virtualization framework (already on every Mac since macOS 12). Boot Alpine + your engine in ~1 second. No installation needed.

On Linux: Just run the binary directly.

1. Download MowisAI.exe / MowisAI.dmg

Feb 20
Of course, let me look up how MCP actually works so we build it correctly.


Searched the web
Searched the web

Good. Now I understand MCP properly. Let me explain it simply then show you exactly what to build.

MCP in plain English:

MCP is an open standard created by Anthropic for connecting AI assistants to external tools and data sources. It's transported over JSON-RPC 2.0 
Cloud Native Now
 — basically structured JSON messages over a connection.

Think of it like this:

Without MCP:                    With MCP:
LLM → custom code → tool A     LLM → MCP → any tool
LLM → custom code → tool B     (one standard protocol)
LLM → custom code → tool C     (plug and play)
You can think of MCP for AI applications the same way a USB-C port works for hardware 
Docker
 — one standard connector for everything.

For MowisAI specifically, here's what the MCP bridge does:

Electron UI
    ↓
MCP Server (your bridge) exposes these tools:
├── shell_exec  → runs command in sandbox
├── file_read   → reads file from sandbox  
├── file_write  → writes file to sandbox
├── file_list   → lists files in sandbox
└── sandbox_create/kill
    ↓
LLM (Claude/GPT) calls these tools
    ↓
Your sandbox engine executes them
The best part: since MCP is a standard, your MowisAI sandbox tools will work with Claude, ChatGPT, Cursor, and any other MCP-compatible AI — not just one LLM.

For v0.1 of the MCP bridge we need:

An MCP server in Node.js (easiest, has official SDK)
Four tools: shell_exec, file_read, file_write, file_list
Each tool talks to your Rust engine via the Unix socket
The MCP server sits between the LLM and your sandbox. Want to start building it?

yeah

Let's set it up. The MCP bridge will be a Node.js project since Anthropic has an official MCP SDK for Node that makes this much easier.

First, check if Node is installed on your Codespace host (not inside the container — on the actual Codespace):

bash
node --version
npm --version
Then create the MCP bridge project alongside your engine:

bash
cd /workspaces/mowisai-engine
mkdir mcp-bridge
cd mcp-bridge
npm init -y
npm install @modelcontextprotocol/sdk
Paste the output. Once that's installed I'll give BlackBox the exact prompt to build the full bridge in one shot — it's actually not that much code since the SDK handles all the MCP protocol stuff, you just define the tools and wire them to your socket.

Feb 20
Before you pull it locally, give BlackBox this exact prompt to build the bridge:

"Create a file called index.js in the current directory. It's an MCP server using @modelcontextprotocol/sdk that exposes 4 tools for controlling a sandbox engine via a Unix socket at /tmp/mowisai.sock.

The socket communication works by sending a newline-terminated JSON object and receiving a JSON response. The request format is: {"task_id": "uuid", "command": "shell command", "timeout_secs": 30, "memory_mb": 512, "cpu_percent": 50} The response format is: {"task_id": "uuid", "status": "done|error", "output": "string"}

Build a helper function sendToEngine(command, timeoutSecs) that connects to the Unix socket, sends the request, waits for response, and returns the output string.

Expose these 4 MCP tools:

shell_exec — input: {command: string, timeout_secs?: number} — sends command to engine, returns output
file_read — input: {path: string} — runs cat {path} in sandbox, returns file contents
file_write — input: {path: string, content: string} — runs echo '{content}' > {path} in sandbox
file_list — input: {path?: string} — runs ls -la {path} in sandbox, returns listing
Use StdioServerTransport from the MCP SDK so it communicates over stdin/stdout. Add a name and version: MowisAI Bridge v0.1.

Also add \"type\": \"module\" to package.json and use ES module imports."

After BlackBox writes it, push to GitHub, pull locally, then we test it by connecting it to Claude Desktop or running it directly. What's your local setup — Windows or Mac?

Two separate issues:

Issue 1 — mowisai-bridge is outside the git repo. It's at /workspaces/mowisai-bridge but your git repo is at /workspaces/mowisai-engine. They're siblings, not nested. So git doesn't know about the bridge folder.

Fix — move the bridge inside the engine repo:

Smart move — OpenRouter gives you access to free models for testing without spending anything. Let's check what free models they have available.


Searched the web
Searched the web

Good news — OpenRouter has a openrouter/free router that automatically selects from available free models and smartly filters for models that support features your request needs like tool calling. 
Docker
 That's perfect for us because we need tool calling support.

Even better — you can append :free to any specific model that has a free variant. 
Docker
 For testing I'd recommend meta-llama/llama-4-maverick:free — it's powerful and supports tool calling.

Here's what you need to do:

Step 1 — Get an OpenRouter API key: Go to openrouter.ai → Sign up → API Keys → Create key. It's free, no credit card needed.

Step 2 — Create a new file in the bridge:

Tell BlackBox:

"Create a file called agent.js in the current directory. It connects an LLM via OpenRouter to the MowisAI MCP bridge tools. It should:

Import axios for HTTP calls
Define the 4 MCP tools as OpenAI-compatible tool definitions (shell_exec, file_read, file_write, file_list)
Have a function callEngine(command) that sends a JSON request to the Unix socket at /tmp/mowisai.sock and returns the output string — same format as the bridge uses
Have a main function runAgent(task) that:
Sends the task to OpenRouter using model meta-llama/llama-4-maverick:free with the 4 tools available
Reads the API key from environment variable OPENROUTER_API_KEY
Loops: if the model responds with tool calls, execute them via callEngine, send results back, continue
If the model responds with plain text, print it and stop
At the bottom call runAgent(process.argv[2]) so you can run it like: node agent.js 'create a file called hello.txt with hello world inside it'"
Then run:

bash
cd /workspaces/mowisai-engine/mowisai-bridge
npm install axios

~280 tps

Tool Use, JSON Object Mode
Meta
Model card
Llama-3.3-70B-Versatile is Meta's advanced multilingual large language model, optimized for a wide range of natural language processing tasks. With 70 billion parameters, it offers high performance across various benchmarks while maintaining efficiency suitable for diverse applications.
PRICING
Input
$0.59
1.7M / $1
Output
$0.79
1.3M / $1
LIMITS
CONTEXT WINDOW
131,072
MAX OUTPUT TOKENS
32,768
QUANTIZATION
This uses Groq's TruePoint Numerics, which reduces precision only in areas that don't affect accuracy, preserving quality while delivering significant speedup over traditional approaches. Learn more here.
Key Technical Specifications
Model Architecture
Built upon Meta's Llama 3.3 architecture, this model utilizes an optimized transformer design with 70 billion parameters. It incorporates Grouped-Query Attention (GQA) to enhance inference scalability and efficiency. The model has been fine-tuned using supervised fine-tuning (SFT) and reinforcement learning with human feedback (RLHF) to align outputs with human preferences for helpfulness and safety.
Performance Metrics
The Llama-3.3-70B-Versatile model demonstrates exceptional performance across multiple benchmarks:
* MMLU (Massive Multitask Language Understanding): 86.0% accuracy
* HumanEval (code generation): 88.4% pass@1
* MATH (mathematical problem solving): 77.0% sympy intersection score
* MGSM (Multilingual Grade School Math): 91.1% exact match
Use Cases
Advanced Language Understanding
Leverage the model's strong multilingual capabilities for complex language understanding tasks across different domains.
Code Generation and Problem Solving
Utilize the model's great performance in code generation, mathematical problem-solving and analytical tasks.
Best Practices
* Clearly specify task instructions and provide sufficient context in your prompts for precise responses.
* Clearly define tool and function definitions for the model to understand their intended use cases, required parameters, expected outputs, and any constraints.
Get Started with Llama-3.3-70B-Versatile
Experience llama-3.3-70b-versatile on Groq:
curlJavaScriptPythonJSON
shell

npm install groq-sdk
JavaScript

import Groq from "groq-sdk";
const groq = new Groq();
async function main() {
  const completion = await groq.chat.completions.create({
    model: "llama-3.3-70b-versatile",
    messages: [
      {
        role: "user",
        content: "Explain why fast inference is critical for reasoning models",
      },
    ],
  });
  console.log(completion.choices[0]?.message?.content);
}
main().catch(console.error);

okay now lets clear things up. look this is the sequence i want look. there is the big sandbox. inside the sanbox there are containers. each container runs an agent, containers share the same  internet network. users can create multiple sandboxes like a sandbox for the frontend team. inside it like 5 agents. they each live in their own container isolated completly but then they share the same network and well create a hub in each sandbox here happens the orchertration. so like all recive the command and hten the agents look at each other there is the coder there is the Ui figma guy there is the artitechure guy and the planner so they work toghet when they communicate they shoul haeve like  cool thing like they have a messages tool so they can talk to each other in private and request files and so on.

Feb 21
That's a genuinely great architecture. Let me draw it out so we're both thinking about the same thing:

MowisAI Platform
├── Sandbox: "Frontend Team"
│   ├── Shared Network (containers talk to each other)
│   ├── Orchestration Hub (receives task, assigns to agents)
│   ├── Container: Planner Agent
│   ├── Container: Architect Agent
│   ├── Container: Coder Agent
│   ├── Container: UI/Figma Agent
│   └── Container: Reviewer Agent
│
└── Sandbox: "Backend Team"
    ├── Shared Network
    ├── Orchestration Hub
    └── Container: agents...
Each agent has tools:

shell_exec, file_read, file_write — what we already built
message_send — send a message to another agent in the same sandbox
message_read — read messages from other agents
file_request — ask another agent for a specific file
task_delegate — assign a subtask to another agent
This is actually a well-known pattern called multi-agent orchestration. What you're describing is very close to how Microsoft AutoGen and CrewAI work — but those run on bare processes. Yours runs each agent in a real isolated container which is significantly more secure and production-ready.

Honest reality check though — what you just described is probably 3-4 weeks of solid work to build properly. Before we jump into it, let me ask you a few things:

The current sandbox has one limitation that blocks everything you just described — containers don't persist. Every task spins up fresh. Agents need to live in a persistent container that stays alive, remembers its state, and can receive messages.

That's the foundation everything else builds on. Should we fix persistent containers first before designing the multi-agent system?

okay lets star by actually focusing on creating the persistant thing

Feb 21
Let's do it. Here's what persistent containers means in practice:

Right now:

task comes in → spawn container → run command → container dies
What we need:

"create agent" → container spawns → stays alive forever
                                  → receives command 1 → runs it
                                  → receives command 2 → runs it (same environment!)
                                  → "kill agent" → container dies
The key change is the container needs a long-running process inside it — basically a mini server that listens for commands, executes them, and sends back output. Instead of spawning a new container per command, we spawn once and send commands to the running container.

Here's the plan. Tell BlackBox:

"In src/container.rs, create a new function called spawn_persistent_container(rootfs_path: &str, session_id: &str, memory_mb: u64, cpu_percent: u64) -> Result<u32> that:

Creates a named Unix socket at /tmp/mowisai-session-{session_id}.sock
Spawns a container using the same namespace isolation as spawn_container_alt
Inside the container, runs a loop that reads newline-delimited JSON commands from the socket, executes them with /bin/sh -c, and writes the output back as JSON
Returns the container PID
Also create exec_in_container(session_id: &str, command: &str, timeout_secs: u64) -> Result<String> that connects to /tmp/mowisai-session-{session_id}.sock, sends the command, and returns the output.

And kill_container(session_id: &str) that kills the process and cleans up the socket."

Then:

bash
cargo build 2>&1 | grep -E "^error|Finished"

Feb 21
Good thinking. Let's design the full structure before writing any code so we know exactly what we're building.

Here's how it maps out:

Sandbox (e.g. "Frontend Team")
├── Orchestration Hub (one per sandbox)
│   ├── Receives the big task from user
│   ├── Breaks it into subtasks
│   ├── Assigns subtasks to agents
│   └── Message bus (agents talk through here)
│
├── Container: Planner Agent
├── Container: Coder Agent  
├── Container: UI Agent
└── Container: Reviewer Agent
What we've already built:

✅ Individual containers with persistent sessions
✅ MCP tools (shell, file read/write)
✅ LLM integration
What we need to build tonight: The orchestration hub and messaging. In practice this is simpler than it sounds — it's just a few new things:

Sandbox manager — a new concept in main.rs that groups sessions together under a sandbox name
Message bus — a simple shared message queue per sandbox, stored in memory. Agents call message_send(to: "coder", message: "here is the plan") and message_read() to get their inbox
Hub agent — a special agent that receives the user task, splits it up, and assigns to other agents
For tonight I'd focus on just the sandbox manager and message bus since those are the foundation. The hub logic comes after.

Want to start?

yeah

Feb 21
Let's build it. First tell BlackBox:

"In src/main.rs, add a Sandbox struct and a global sandbox registry. The Sandbox struct should have:

name: String
sessions: HashMap<String, String> — maps agent_name to session_id
messages: HashMap<String, Vec<Message>> — inbox per agent
The Message struct should have:

from: String
to: String
content: String
timestamp: u64
Use Arc<Mutex<HashMap<String, Sandbox>>> as the global registry so it's thread safe.

Add handling for these new request types in the main socket handler:

create_sandbox — takes sandbox_name, creates a new Sandbox, returns ok
join_sandbox — takes sandbox_name and agent_name, creates a persistent session and registers it in the sandbox
message_send — takes sandbox_name, from_agent, to_agent, content, adds to recipient's inbox
message_read — takes sandbox_name, agent_name, returns all messages in that agent's inbox and clears it"

cargo build 2>&1 | grep -E "^error|Finished"

Feb 21
What do you want to tackle next? The natural options are:

Orchestration hub — the special agent that receives a user task, breaks it into subtasks, and assigns them to the right agents. This is what makes it a real multi-agent system.
Wire the agent loop to sessions — right now agent.js uses one-shot exec mode. Update it to use persistent sessions so the LLM agent actually lives in a container and remembers state between tool calls.
Streaming output — see the agent's output in real time instead of waiting for the full response.
I'd personally go with option 2 first since it connects everything we built today — sessions, sandboxes, messaging — to the actual LLM agent. Without it the agent doesn't really "live" in a container, it just fires commands at one.

What do you think?

Let's wire the agent to use persistent sessions. Tell BlackBox:

"In agent.js, add session management to the agent loop. At the start of runAgent:

First send a create_sandbox request to the engine socket with a generated sandbox name like sandbox-{uuid}
Then send a join_sandbox request with agent_name: 'main-agent' to get a persistent session
Store the session_id from the response
Change the callEngine function to use run_in_session request type instead of exec, passing the session_id
At the end when the task is complete, send a kill_session request to clean up
The socket communication format is the same as before — newline delimited JSON to /tmp/mowisai.sock."
