Almost! The agent is planning the tool calls correctly but not actually executing them — it's printing the function calls as text instead of running them. The tool execution loop isn't triggering.

Tell BlackBox:

"In agent.js, the tool execution loop is broken. The agent is returning tool calls but they're not being executed. Check the response handling — after calling groq.chat.completions.create, check if response.choices[0].message.tool_calls exists and has items. If it does, loop through each tool call, execute it via callEngine, collect the results, then send them back to Groq as role: 'tool' messages. Keep looping until the model returns a plain text response with no tool calls."

March 13 is confirmed. Here's exactly how the process works:

If you applied before February 9th:

You will get a decision by March 13. If your application is promising, they will invite you to interview. Most interviews are held by video conference in February and March. They typically make decisions the same day as the interview. 
Cloud Native Now
The steps between now and money:

March 13 or before — you either get an interview invite or a rejection
Interview — ~10 minute video call with YC partners, very fast paced
Same day decision — they tell you yes or no on the spot
If accepted — they invest in companies as soon as they are accepted, they do not wait for the batch to start 
Cloud Native Now
April — batch starts in San Francisco, 3 day kickoff
Demo Day (June) — you pitch to investors for your next round
The investment itself: accepted companies receive an immediate investment of $500,000 
Docker
 via a SAFE agreement.

So to answer your question directly — yes, if accepted there's still an interview step first before any money. The decision comes same day as the interview though, so it moves fast.

What is your company going to make? Please describe your product and what it does or will do.
MominAI is a native, low-level runtime that lets AI agents run safely and autonomously in isolated sandboxes. Each agent has its own CPU, RAM, storage, memory, and controlled tool access, and can spawn sub-agents with smaller sandboxes. Our platform acts like Docker for AI, enabling developers to safely execute, manage, and scale autonomous agents locally or in hybrid environments while preserving full isolation and governance.
Where do you live now, and where would the company be based after YC?
Tampere, Finland/ Tampere, Finland
Explain your decision regarding location.
I am still young and i belive that Tampere is a great place to run the company. Tampere and Finland in general has one of the cheapest electricity in the world that makes running things like LLMs and AI agents in our infrastructure.
Progress
How far along are you?
We are great along, currently the main isolation and and containirizaition engines are built and are working currently we need to add the other tools like RAG, vectorDBs, and some other tools. So the main rust and c coding is almost done but the top level software still has to be built.
How long have each of you been working on this? How much of that has been full-time? Please explain.
I have been working around 5-6 hours a day after school, at night and in the morning, and at school when i have time.
What tech stack are you using, or planning to use, to build this product? Include AI models and AI coding tools you use.
Core Runtime / Sandbox  C – for low-level OS interactions, memory and CPU management, sandboxing primitives, and performance-critical code.  Rust – for safe systems-level components, concurrency, and secure memory management. Great for preventing common security bugs in agent isolation.  C++ for performance libraries if needed, but mainly C + Rust is enough.  2. Agent APIs & High-Level Layer  TypeScript / Node.js – for agent orchestration, tool registration, communication channels, and developer-facing SDKs.  Python – for AI model integration (LLMs, embedding libraries, ML tools). Allows compatibility with most existing AI frameworks.  gRPC or REST – for communication between agent sandboxes and high-level orchestration layer.  3. Storage & Memory  SQLite / LMDB – lightweight, embedded database for agent buckets and persistent memory.  Vector databases (e.g., Weaviate, Pinecone) – optional, for semantic retrieval in long-term memory.  4. Optional GPU / ML Acceleration  CUDA / ROCm – if subagents or tools need GPU inference locally.  ONNX Runtime / TensorRT – to run models efficiently in sandboxed environments.  5. Orchestration & Dev Tools  CLI tools in Rust or Go – to spawn sandboxes, manage resources, inspect agents.  Frida / Seccomp / Linux namespaces – for sandbox isolation if on Linux.  Cross-platform support – Windows, macOS, Linux with abstraction for system calls.

Feb 22
Perfect — these updates are exactly what can save your application. Let me write each field for you properly.

How far along are you?

We have a fully working v0.1 sandbox engine. In the past 3 weeks we built: a Rust-based container runtime using real Linux namespaces (PID, mount, IPC, UTS) for isolation, cgroups v2 for per-container RAM and CPU limits, persistent agent sessions that maintain state between commands, a multi-agent sandbox system where multiple agents run in separate containers but share a network, an agent-to-agent messaging system, and an MCP bridge that connects any LLM to the sandbox tools. We tested it with Llama 3.3 70B via Groq — the agent autonomously wrote a Python fibonacci script and executed it inside the sandbox. The core infrastructure is done. We are now building the orchestration layer and Electron UI.

How long have you been working on this?

I have been working on this for approximately 3 weeks, roughly 4-6 hours per day after school and on weekends. The core sandbox engine, MCP bridge, persistent sessions, and multi-agent messaging were all built in under 12 hours of active coding across 3 days.

Tech stack:

Core engine: Rust (Linux namespace isolation, cgroups v2 resource limits, Unix socket server). Container base: Alpine Linux 3.19 with Node.js 20 and Python 3.11 pre-installed. Agent protocol: MCP (Model Context Protocol) via Node.js SDK. LLM integration: Groq API (Llama 3.3 70B). Frontend: Electron (in progress). The engine runs as a single binary with no Docker daemon dependency.

Are people using your product? → No

When will you have a version people can use?

We have a working CLI version now. A usable Electron desktop app is 4-6 weeks away.

For the demo video — this is the most important thing. Can you record a 2-3 minute screen recording showing:

The agent receiving a task
It writing and executing code in the sandbox
The output coming back
That video alone could change everything. Can you record one today even if you're sick?

Feb 22
Let's go. The orchestration hub is the brain of the whole system.

Here's what it needs to do:

User gives task: "Build a React login page"
        ↓
Hub Agent receives it
        ↓
Hub breaks it into subtasks:
  - Planner: "Create the project structure and plan"
  - Coder: "Write the React components"
  - Reviewer: "Check the code for bugs"
        ↓
Hub assigns each subtask via message_send
        ↓
Agents work, report back via message_send to hub
        ↓
Hub collects results and reports to user
Tell BlackBox:

"Create a file called orchestrator.js in the mowisai-bridge directory. It should:

Import groq-sdk and the socket communication code from agent.js (or duplicate the sendToEngine function)
Have a list of specialist agents: planner, coder, reviewer
On startup: create a sandbox, spawn all 3 agents into it using join_sandbox, plus a hub agent
Have a function assignTask(agentName, task) that sends a message via message_send to that agent
Have a function readInbox(agentName) that reads messages via message_read
The main runOrchestrator(userTask) function should:
Send the user task to Groq with a system prompt saying it is the hub orchestrator and must break the task into subtasks and assign them to planner, coder, and reviewer
Parse the LLM response to get subtask assignments
Call assignTask for each agent
Poll each agent's inbox every 2 seconds waiting for their response messages
Once all agents respond, send all results back to Groq for a final summary
Print the final summary
At the bottom call runOrchestrator(process.argv[2])"

Feb 22
The orchestration is working beautifully — sandbox created, 4 agents spawned, hub planned the tasks, assigned subtasks to each agent. The problem is it's stuck in an infinite polling loop waiting for agents to reply, but nobody is replying because the agents are passive — they just have messages in their inbox but nothing is processing them.

The fix: the orchestrator itself needs to act as each agent, read their inbox, call Groq for each agent's response, then send results back to hub.

Tell BlackBox:

"In orchestrator.js, after assigning subtasks, add an executeAgentTask function that:

Calls message_read for the given agent to get their assigned task
Sends that task to Groq with a system prompt like 'You are the {agentName} agent. Complete your assigned task and respond with your result.'
Sends the LLM response back to hub via message_send from that agent to hub
After assigning all subtasks, call executeAgentTask for planner, coder, and reviewer in parallel using Promise.all. Then read hub's inbox to collect all 3 results and send to Groq for final summary. Remove the polling loop entirely."

1:31 PM
Two problems:

Infinite loop — the LLM keeps calling echo hello over and over instead of stopping. The agent loop isn't detecting when the task is done.
CLI formatting — still mixing engine logs with CLI output.
Let's fix the loop issue first since it's more critical. Tell BlackBox:

"In agent.js, the agent loop is stuck in an infinite loop. Add a maximum iteration limit of 10 tool calls. After 10 tool calls, break out of the loop and return whatever result we have. Also add this check: if the LLM returns the exact same tool call with the exact same arguments as the previous iteration, break immediately — it means the model is stuck in a loop."

Then for the engine log noise mixing with CLI output, tell BlackBox:

"In cli.js, redirect the engine's stdout logs away from the CLI output. When spawning the agent process, pipe its stderr and stdout separately so engine debug logs don't pollute the CLI display."

After both fixes:

bash
sudo -E node cli.js run "echo hello"

  cyan: chalk.cyan,
  green: pastedi am really sorry but this is something you need to fix so here is the cli.js1:48 PMNo worries, I can see the issue clearly from the code.
The problem: In runAgent(), you're intercepting console.log to capture output — but the engine itself also uses console.log for all those 🔗 📨 ▶️ logs. So they all get captured and dumped into agentOutput, then printed in the final result box. That's why you see the massive wall of engine noise in the final output.
The double-agent problem: The agent module is logging with [Agent] to stderr, but the engine logs go to stdout — and since cli.js imports agent.js directly (in-process), the engine's net.createServer stdout is shared. When the orchestrator also starts up, it creates a second sandbox.
Here's the fixed cli.js runAgent function — replace just that section:
jsasync function runAgent(task) {
  if (!task) {
    printStatus('No task provided', 'error');
    console.log('Usage: node cli.js run "your task here"');
    process.exit(1);
  }

  try {
    printHeader();
    printDivider();
    printStatus('Starting single agent mode', 'info');
    printDivider();

    // Import agent module directly
    const agentModule = await import('./agent.js');

    // Engine noise patterns to filter out
    const enginePatterns = ['🔗', '📨', '▶️', '✅', '👋', '🏖️', '🤖', '💀', '🔍', '📡', '🚀', '❌'];

    const originalLog = console.log;
    let finalResult = '';

    // Intercept console.log — show [Agent] lines as status, suppress engine noise
    console.log = (...args) => {
      const message = args.join(' ');

      // Suppress engine noise
      if (enginePatterns.some(p => message.includes(p))) return;

      // Show [Agent] lines as live status updates
      if (message.includes('[Agent]')) {
        const clean = message.replace('[Agent] ', '');
        if (clean.includes('error') || clean.includes('Error')) {
          printStatus(clean, 'error');
        } else if (clean.includes('Executing') || clean.includes('Waiting')) {
          printStatus(clean, 'warning');
        } else {
          printStatus(clean, 'info');
        }
        return;
      }

      // Capture final result output (non-noise, non-agent-log lines)
      finalResult += message + '\n';
    };

    await agentModule.runAgent(task);

    console.log = originalLog;

    printDivider();
    printFinalResult(finalResult.trim() || 'Task completed successfully');

  } catch (err) {
    printStatus(err.message, 'error');
    process.exit(1);
  }
}
The key changes:

Engine emoji lines are silently dropped
[Agent] lines are shown as live status updates while the task runs
Only the actual task result (non-noise lines) goes into the final result box
No more double-agent since we're not touching the orchestrator at all
